'''Portugal''',{{efn|{{IPA|PT-pt|puɾtuˈɣal|audio=Portugal.wav|help=no}}}} officially the '''Portuguese Republic''',{{efn|{{langx|PT-pt|República Portuguesa|links=no}}, {{IPA|PT-pt|ʁɛˈpuβlikɐ puɾtuˈgezɐ|IPA|help=no}}}} is a country located on the [[Iberian Peninsula]] in [[Southwestern Europe]]. It is a [[Unitary state|unitary republic]] made up by [[Continental Portugal|mainland Portugal]] and two [[Autonomous Regions of Portugal|autonomous regions]], with [[Lisbon]] as its [[Capital city|capital]] and [[List of largest cities in Portugal|largest city]]. It has a [[Demographics of Portugal|population]] of over 10.7 million. The mainland includes [[Cabo da Roca|the westernmost point]] of [[continental Europe]] and is [[Portugal-Spain border|bordered]] by [[Spain]] to the north and east; [[Madeira]] and the [[Azores]] are in the [[Atlantic Ocean]].

The Western Iberian Peninsula has been inhabited since [[Prehistoric Iberia|prehistoric times]], with the earliest signs of [[Human settlement|settlement]] dating to 5500 BC. Portugal was established as a [[County of Portugal|county]] of the [[Kingdom of León]] in 868, and [[Manifestis Probatum|formally]] as a [[Kingdom of Portugal|kingdom]] in {{YEAR|1179}}, resulting from the {{lang|pt-PT|[[Portugal in the Reconquista|Reconquista]]}} against the Muslims, who had [[Al-Andalus|occupied]] the Iberian Peninsula [[Battle of Guadalete|since 711]]. A major participant in the [[Age of Discovery]], the kingdom saw seminal advancements in [[Portuguese nautical science|nautical science]], resulting in [[Portuguese maritime exploration|maritime exploration]] to discover new territories and sea routes, which led to the establishment of the [[Portuguese Empire]]. The kingdom [[5 October 1910 revolution|became a republic]] in {{YEAR|1910}} and was a dictatorship [[28 May 1926 coup d'état|from 1926]] until [[Carnation Revolution|the dictatorship's overthrow]] in {{YEAR|1974}} [[Portuguese transition to democracy|enabled]] the [[Constitution of Portugal|full establishment of democracy]] in {{YEAR|1976}}.

Portugal is a [[Semi-presidential system|semi-presidential]] constitutional unitary republic and [[Multi-party system|multi-party]] [[representative democracy]] with four separate [[Sovereignty body (Portugal)|sovereignty bodies]]: [[President of Portugal|president]], [[Government of Portugal|government]], parliament, and [[Judiciary of Portugal|judiciary]]. It has a [[Unicameralism|unicameral national legislature]] known as the [[Assembly of the Republic (Portugal)|Assembly of the Republic]]. Portugal has developed a [[Administrative divisions of Portugal|complex system]] to manage its territory, even though the mainland continues to remain [[Centralized government|highly centralised]].

A [[developed country]], Portugal has an [[IMF advanced economy list|advanced economy]] that chiefly relies upon services, industry, and [[Tourism in Portugal|tourism]]. Shaped by the various civilisations that have inhabitted its territory, Portugal developed a [[Culture of Portugal|culture]] with [[Evolution of the Portuguese Empire|a worldwide influence]] that allowed [[Portuguese language|Portuguese]] to become the world's [[List of languages by number of native speakers|fifth-most spoken native language]] with more than 250 million [[Lusophone|native speakers]]. A [[International organization membership of Portugal|member of numerous international organizations]], Portugal maintains an active role in international political, cultural, economic, and military affairs.

== Etymology ==

The word {{anchor|Name|Etymology}} ''Portugal'' derives from [[Latin]] {{lang|la|[[Portus Cale]]}}, meaning 'port of {{lang|la|Cale}}', itself the origin of [[Porto]]; ''Cale'' was a town on the [[Douro]]. The usual explanation is that it is an [[ethnonym]] derived from the [[Gallaeci]] peoples, who occupied the north-west of the [[Iberian Peninsula]].<ref>{{cite book|pages=76–79|publication-place=Brighton|publisher=Sussex Academic Press|publication-date=2022|oclc=1227270763|isbn=978-1-78976-104-7|title=The global history of Portugal : from prehistory to the modern world|series=The Portuguese-Speaking World|editor-first1=Carlos D.|editor-last1=Fiolhais|editor-first2=José Eduardo|editor-last2=Franco|editor-first3=José Pedro|editor-last3=Paiva|first1=Pedro C.|last1=Carvalho|chapter=409–411 — Arrivals of the Barbarians}}</ref><ref>{{Cite conference |url=https://d1wqtxts1xzle7.cloudfront.net/64928298/Origem_e_significado_do_nome_de_Portugal_e_a_Galiza_FINAL1-libre.pdf?1605294355=&response-content-disposition=inline%3B+filename%3DOrigem_e_significado_dos_nomes_de_Portug.pdf&Expires=1772315413&Signature=fCikagvlPJT~wqaUY~HVr0iIP-Do7afNyeQj73g8GYcO1SKnhN~LOpumxxlJEvD53LN3dl5vQsYvEMzQlfZ4N~spi~npQzEPgTDTex8acDmj9hXawxoCG3yzJvM~PdyCSyg6dxGGVCbV4lAaZSp3BkD2FDV60-c589-r8KpmUh0yElyyCMtGFyGbonz8kEJIUYVDcVPHS-7c3o7SQk2yYMkgiI1f4VuD7ZzBAGHT8dUAbcrjAl4J6be3HupGG1pvhxIjDR3sF~EGswOR04SS0~W4F2BRPfpU1dq1~xmvphqOWtp-jMSFyqEVrkTCEjpxjpruSeUrKNDaGnwRbQ02ZQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAJLOHF5GGSLRBV4ZA|via=Academia.edu|title=Origem e significado dos nomes de Portugal e Galiza|book-title=Actas do III Congreso Internacional sobre a cultura celta: Os celtas da Europa atlántica|conference=III Congresso Internacional sobre a Cultura Celta: Os Celtas da Europa Atlântica|trans-title=Origin and meaning of the names of Portugal and Galicia|language=pt-PT|location=Narón, Galicia|first=Luís|last=Magarinhos|access-date=28 February 2026|url-status=live}}</ref>{{efn|One theory proposes ''Cale'' is a derivation of the Celtic word for 'port'.<ref>{{cite book nations|publication-place=London|oclc=929427027|access-date=22 August 2010 |isbn=978-0-415-20479-8 |last1=Pezron |first1=Paul |date=2000|via=Google Books |publisher=Routledge |archive-date=10 October 2023 |page=270|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20231010145948/https://books.google.com/books?id=POGEbFpGHigC&q=celtic+cale+harbour&pg=RA1-PA270 |url-status=live }}</ref> Another is that ''Cala'' was a Celtic goddess, or that it may have come from {{Lang|la|Portus Gallus}}, 'port of the Gauls'.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=MD8zAQAAMAAJ&q=portus+Gallus+etymologie+du+portugal&pg=PA441|title=Manuel géographique et statistique de l'Espagne et du Portugal Buisson|via=Google Books|access-date=17 November 2020|archive-date=2 February 2024|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240202203433/https://books.google.com/books?id=MD8zAQAAMAAJ&q=portus+Gallus+etymologie+du+portugal&pg=PA441#v=snippet&q=portus%20Gallus%20etymologie%20du%20portugal&f=false|url-status=live}}</ref>}}

Around 136{{nbsp}}BC, the [[Ancient Rome|Romans]], during the [[Second Punic War]], conquered Cale from the [[Punic people|Carthaginians]] and renamed it {{Lang|la|Portus Cale}}.<ref>{{Cite conference |url=https://d1wqtxts1xzle7.cloudfront.net/64928298/Origem_e_significado_do_nome_de_Portugal_e_a_Galiza_FINAL1-libre.pdf?1605294355=&response-content-disposition=inline%3B+filename%3DOrigem_e_significado_dos_nomes_de_Portug.pdf&Expires=1772315413&Signature=fCikagvlPJT~wqaUY~HVr0iIP-Do7afNyeQj73g8GYcO1SKnhN~LOpumxxlJEvD53LN3dl5vQsYvEMzQlfZ4N~spi~npQzEPgTDTex8acDmj9hXawxoCG3yzJvM~PdyCSyg6dxGGVCbV4lAaZSp3BkD2FDV60-c589-r8KpmUh0yElyyCMtGFyGbonz8kEJIUYVDcVPHS-7c3o7SQk2yYMkgiI1f4VuD7ZzBAGHT8dUAbcrjAl4J6be3HupGG1pvhxIjDR3sF~EGswOR04SS0~W4F2BRPfpU1dq1~xmvphqOWtp-jMSFyqEVrkTCEjpxjpruSeUrKNDaGnwRbQ02ZQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAJLOHF5GGSLRBV4ZA|title=Origem e significado dos nomes de Portugal e Galiza|book-title=Actas do III Congreso Internacional sobre a cultura celta: Os celtas da Europa atlántica|conference=III Congresso Internacional sobre a Cultura Celta: Os Celtas da Europa Atlântica|trans-title=Origin and meaning of the names of Portugal and Galicia|language=pt-PT|location=Narón, Galicia|first=Luís|last=Magarinhos|access-date=28 February 2026|page=543|date=15 April 2011|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20260305150327/https://www.academia.edu/10819665/Origem_e_significado_dos_nomes_de_Portugal_e_da_Galiza_Lu%C3%ADs_Magarinhos|archive-date=5 March 2026|url-status=live|via=Academia.edu}}</ref><ref name="namesPorto">{{cite journal|title=Ocupação da época romana na cidade do Porto. Ponto de situação e perspectivas de pesquisa|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160614065759/https://dialnet.unirioja.es/descarga/articulo/3282795.pdf|archive-date=14 June 2016|journal=Gallaecia: Revista de arqueoloxía e antigüidade|pages=213–262|issn=0211-8653|date=2010|url=https://dialnet.unirioja.es/descarga/articulo/3282795.pdf|access-date=26 April 2026|volume=29|publisher=Universidade de Santiago de Compostela|publication-place= Santiago de Compostela|first=António Manuel S. P.|last=Silva|language=pt-PT|trans-title=Roman-period occupation in the city of Porto: current state of knowledge and research perspectives}}</ref> During the [[Middle Ages]], the region around Portus Cale became known by the [[Visigoths]] as ''Portucale'' and by the [[Suebi]] as ''Parochiale''.<ref name="namesPorto" /> The name ''Portucale'' evolved into ''Portugale''; by the 11th and 12th centuries ''Portugal'' was used to refer to the region between the Douro and [[Minho River|Minho]] rivers.<ref>{{cite book|title=Being Portuguese in Spanish: Reimagining Early Modern Iberian Literature, 1580-1640|page=25|volume=78|series=Purdue Studies in Romance Literatures|publisher=Purdue University Press|publication-place=West Lafayette, Indiana|isbn=978-1-55753-884-0|publication-date=April 2020|last=Wade|first=Jonathan William|url=https://docs.lib.purdue.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1049&context=purduepress_previews|access-date=27 April 2026|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220526110634/https://docs.lib.purdue.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1049&context=purduepress_previews|archive-date=26 May 2022|url-status=live}}</ref>

== History ==
{{Main|History of Portugal}}

=== Prehistory ===
{{Main|Prehistoric Iberia}}

The region has been inhabited by humans since approximately 400,000 years ago.{{efn|The earliest human trace found in Portugal is the 400,000-year-old [[Aroeira 3]], a {{lang|la|[[Homo heidelbergensis]]}} skull discovered in the [[Cave of Aroeira]] in 2014.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Daura |first1=Joan |last2=Sanz |first2=Montserrat |last3=Arsuaga |first3=Juan Luis |last4=Hoffmann |first4=Dirk L. |last5=Quam |first5=Rolf M. |last6=Ortega |first6=María Cruz |last7=Santos |first7=Elena |last8=Gómez |first8=Sandra |last9=Rubio |first9=Angel |last10=Villaescusa |first10=Lucía |last11=Souto |first11=Pedro |last12=Mauricio |first12=João |last13=Rodrigues |first13=Filipa |last14=Ferreira |first14=Artur |last15=Godinho |first15=Paulo |last16=Trinkaus |first16=Erik |last17=Zilhão |first17=João |title=New Middle Pleistocene hominin cranium from Gruta da Aroeira (Portugal) |journal=Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences |date=28 March 2017 |volume=114 |issue=13 |pages=3397–3402 |doi=10.1073/pnas.1619040114 |doi-access=free |pmid=28289213 |pmc=5380066 |bibcode=2017PNAS..114.3397D }}</ref>}} [[Neanderthal]]s roamed the northern Iberian peninsula, and a [[hominini|hominin]] tooth has been found at the Nova da Columbeira Cave in [[Estremadura Province (1936–76)|Estremadura]].<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Zilhão |first1=João |last2=Cardoso |first2=João Luís |last3=Pike |first3=Alistair W. G. |last4=Weninger |first4=Bernhard |title=Gruta Nova da Columbeira (Bombarral, Portugal): Site stratigraphy, age of the Mousterian sequence, and implications for the timing of Neanderthal extinction in Iberia |trans-title=Gruta Nova da Columbeira (Bombarral, Portugal): Site stratigraphy, age of the Mousterian sequence, and implications for the timing of Neanderthal extinction in Iberia |language=pt |journal=Quartär |date=20 October 2021 |volume=58 |pages=93–112 |doi=10.7485/QU58_05 |doi-access=free }}</ref> {{lang|la|[[Homo sapiens sapiens]]}} arrived in what is now Portugal around 35,000 years ago and spread rapidly,<ref>{{cite book|pages=5|isbn=978-0-511-65027-7|publisher=Cambridge University Press|oclc=559058693|publication-place=Cambridge|first=Anthony R.|last=Disney|url=https://fenix.iseg.ulisboa.pt/downloadFile/1126033050836544/A.%20R.%20Disney%20-%20A%20History%20of%20Portugal%20and%20the%20Portuguese%20Empire_%20From%20Beginnings%20to%201807%20%28Volume%201%29%20%282009%29.pdf|publication-date=June 2009|access-date=18 April 2026|title=A History of Portugal and the Portuguese Empire|volume=1: Portugal|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20251116214058/https://fenix.iseg.ulisboa.pt/downloadFile/1126033050836544/A.%20R.%20Disney%20-%20A%20History%20of%20Portugal%20and%20the%20Portuguese%20Empire_%20From%20Beginnings%20to%201807%20(Volume%201)%20(2009).pdf|url-status=live|archive-date=16 November 2025|via=[[University of Lisbon]]}}</ref> with the onset of the [[Neolithic]] dating to approximately 5400 BC.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Zilhão |first1=João |title=Radiocarbon evidence for maritime pioneer colonization at the origins of farming in west Mediterranean Europe |journal=Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences |date=20 November 2001 |volume=98 |issue=24 |pages=14180–14185 |doi=10.1073/pnas.241522898 |pmid=11707599 |doi-access=free |pmc=61188 |bibcode=2001PNAS...9814180Z }}</ref> In southern Portugal, [[Iron Age Europe|Iron Age]] inscribed [[stele]] have been found, representing the earliest known evidence of writing on the Iberian Peninsula.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Luján Martínez |first1=Eugenio |title=El sudoeste de la Península Ibérica |trans-title=The Southwest of the Iberian Peninsula |language=es |journal=Palaeohispanica. Revista sobre lenguas y culturas de la Hispania Antigua |date=1 May 2020 |issue=20 |pages=561–589 |doi=10.36707/palaeohispanica.v0i20.394 |doi-access=free }}</ref> Pre-Celtic tribes inhabited Portugal and the [[Lusitanians]] occupied central, inland regions. [[Celts]] mainly inhabited the north and centre of Portugal, leaving lasting traces in the language and culture.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Sims-Williams |first1=Patrick |title=An Alternative to 'Celtic from the East' and 'Celtic from the West' |journal=Cambridge Archaeological Journal |date=August 2020 |volume=30 |issue=3 |pages=511–529 |doi=10.1017/S0959774320000098 |doi-access=free }}</ref>

=== Antiquity and early Middle Ages ===
{{Main|Lusitania|Gallaecia|Hispania}}
[[File:Roman Temple Evora Portugal 02.jpg|thumb|upright 0.85|The [[Roman Temple of Évora]], built in the 1st century|alt=Ruins of the Roman Temple of Évora with tall Corinthian columns standing on a raised platform made of rocks in a sunlit town square, set against a bright blue sky, with a medieval tower and white buildings in the background.]]

Romans first [[Second Punic War|invaded the Iberian Peninsula]] in 218{{spaces}}BC.<ref>{{cite book |last1=Richardson |first1=J. S. |chapter=Spain and Roman Imperialism |pages=1–10 |title=Hispaniae |date=1986 |doi=10.1017/CBO9780511518591 |isbn=978-0-521-32183-9 |oclc=958552123 }}</ref> The Carthaginians, Rome's opponent in the [[Punic Wars]], were expelled from the peninsula in 206{{spaces}}BC after the [[Battle of Ilipa]].<ref>{{cite book |last1=Zimmermann |first1=Klaus |title=A Companion to the Punic Wars |chapter=Roman Strategy and Aims in the Second Punic War |date=2011 |pages=280–298 |doi=10.1002/9781444393712.ch16 |isbn=978-1-4051-7600-2 |oclc=657223828 |lccn=2010033794 }}</ref><ref>{{cite book|last=Miles|first=Richard|author-link= Richard Miles (historian)|title=Carthage must be destroyed: the rise and fall of an ancient civilization|location=London|publisher=Penguin|year=2013|isbn=978-0-14-101809-6|page=303|oclc=826591134}}</ref> Within 200 years, almost the entire Iberian Peninsula had been annexed to the [[Roman Republic]], facing [[Lusitanian Wars|resistance]] from local tribes, notably the Lusitanians led by [[Viriathus]].<ref>{{cite book|last1=Keay|first1=Simon J.|title=Roman Spain|date=1988|publisher=University of California Press|page=25|oclc=17300435|isbn=0520063805|lccn=87035722|publication-place=Berkeley, California}}</ref><ref>{{cite book|pages=5–9|publication-place=Cambridge|publisher=Cambridge University Press|publication-date=1947|title=A History Of Portugal|url=https://archive.org/details/in.ernet.dli.2015.152869|access-date=16 April 2026|last=Livermore|first=H. V.|via=[[Wayback Machine]]}}</ref>

In 409, with the decline of the [[Western Roman Empire|Roman Empire]], the [[Iberian Peninsula]] was invaded by [[Germanic tribes]].<ref>{{cite book|pages=34–37|publication-place=Cambridge|isbn=978-0-511-65027-7|publisher=Cambridge University Press|first=Anthony R.|last=Disney|url=https://fenix.iseg.ulisboa.pt/downloadFile/1126033050836544/A.%20R.%20Disney%20-%20A%20History%20of%20Portugal%20and%20the%20Portuguese%20Empire_%20From%20Beginnings%20to%201807%20%28Volume%201%29%20%282009%29.pdf|publication-date=June 2009|access-date=18 April 2026|title=A History of Portugal and the Portuguese Empire|oclc=559058693|volume=1: Portugal|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20251116214058/https://fenix.iseg.ulisboa.pt/downloadFile/1126033050836544/A.%20R.%20Disney%20-%20A%20History%20of%20Portugal%20and%20the%20Portuguese%20Empire_%20From%20Beginnings%20to%201807%20(Volume%201)%20(2009).pdf|url-status=live|archive-date=16 November 2025|via=[[University of Lisbon]]}}</ref> Western Iberia was integrated into the [[Suebian Kingdom]], with its capital at [[Braga]].<ref>{{cite book |last1=Sánchez |first1=Fernando López |title=Culture and Society in Medieval Galicia |chapter=The Suevic Kingdom: Why Gallaecia? |publication-date=28 July 2015|publication-place=Leiden |editor-first=James|editor-last=D'Emilio|pages=176–209 |doi=10.1163/9789004288607_006 |isbn=978-90-04-28860-7|series=The Medieval and Early Modern Iberian World|volume=58|publisher=Brill|oclc=914709949}}</ref> The [[Visigoths]] defeated the [[Suebi]] and ruled the peninsula until the early 8th century.<ref>{{cite book |last1=Lenski |first1=Noel |title=Slavery in the Late Antique World, 150 – 700 CE |chapter=Slavery among the Visigoths |date=2022 |pages=251–280 |doi=10.1017/9781108568159.014 |isbn=978-1-108-56815-9 }}</ref><ref>{{cite book |last1=Collins |first1=Roger |title=Spain |chapter=Visigothic Spain 409–711 |date=2000 |pages=39–62 |doi=10.1093/oso/9780198206194.003.0003 |isbn=978-0-19-820619-4 }}</ref> 

In 711, the Iberian Peninsula was [[Muslim conquest of the Iberian Peninsula|invaded from the south]] by the [[Umayyad Caliphate]], who expanded rapidly in the peninsula as most became part of [[al-Andalus]] in 726.<ref>{{cite book|page=310|url=https://archive.org/details/historyofislamic0000lapi_t2d2|access-date=19 April 2026|publication-place=Cambridge|publisher=Cambridge University Press|title=A history of Islamic societies|edition=2nd|oclc=920280085|year=2002|via=Wayback Machine|isbn=9780521779333|first=Ira Marvin|last=Lapidus}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal |last1=Leitão |first1=André de Oliveira |date=2008 |title=Do Garb al-Ândalus ao «segundo reino» da «Coroa de Portugal»: território, política e identidade |trans-title=From Ġarb al-Ândalus to the 'second kingdom' of the 'Crown of Portugal': territory, politics and identity |language=pt-PT |journal=Clio |volume=16/17 |pages=69–104 |hdl=10451/38199 |hdl-access=free|issn=0870-4104|publisher=University of Lisbon Centre for History|publication-place=Lisbon|url=https://repositorio.ulisboa.pt/bitstreams/77a9138d-0374-4bab-a77a-6ff1e3da9021/download|access-date=18 April 2026|type=Journal article}}</ref><ref>{{Cite book|via=Google Books|access-date=28 April 2026|editor-last=Rogers|editor-first=Clifford J.|first=J. M. B.|last=Porter|url-access=limited|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=mzwpq6bLHhMC&q=Tariq+ibn+Ziyad+berber+arab+troops&pg=PA13|page=13|title=The Oxford encyclopedia of medieval warfare and military technology|year=2010|publisher=Oxford University Press|isbn=978-0-19-533403-6|lccn=2009042630|oclc=437300064|publication-place=New York|volume=1|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20241202145105/https://books.google.com/books?id=mzwpq6bLHhMC&q=Tariq+ibn+Ziyad+berber+arab+troops&pg=PA13#v=snippet&q=Tariq%20ibn%20Ziyad%20berber%20arab%20troops&f=false|archive-date=2 December 2024|url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{Cite book|editor-last=Esposito|editor-first=John L.|chapter=Chapter 1: Muhammad and the Caliphate|first=Fred M.|last=Donner|url=https://archive.org/details/oxfordhistoryofi00john|title=The Oxford History of Islam|year=1999|publication-place=New York|oclc=1406978184|lccn=99-13219|access-date=18 April 2026|publisher=Oxford University Press|isbn=9780195107999|page=21|via=Wayback Machine}}</ref> It was part of the Umayyad Caliphate until the caliphate's collapse in 750<ref>{{cite book|year=2011|publisher=Liverpool University Press|page=133|edition=2nd|volume=9|publication-place=Liverpool|isbn=978-0-85323-554-5|series=Translated Texts for Historians|title=Conquerors and Chroniclers of Early Medieval Spain|first=Kenneth Baxter|last=Wolf|oclc=783942572}}</ref> when the western part of the caliphate gained its independence under [[Abd al-Rahman I]] and the establishment of the [[Emirate of Córdoba]].{{efn|Ater 929, the [[Caliphate of Córdoba]]<ref>{{cite book |last1=Barton |first1=Simon|lccn=2003066374|oclc=58477225|publication-place=Basingstoke|title=A History of Spain |date=2004 |publisher=Palgrave Macmillan |isbn=978-0-333-63257-4|edition=1st|page=38}}</ref><ref>{{cite book |first=Bernard F. |last=Reilly |title=The Medieval Spains |publication-place=Cambridge |publisher=Cambridge University Press|location=New York, NY, USA|year=1993|isbn=978-0-521-39436-9|series=Cambridge Medieval Textbooks|lccn=92023379|page=84}}</ref>}} This lasted until 1031, with its dissolution into 23 [[Taifa|small kingdoms]],<ref>{{cite book |via=Google Books|url-access=limited|last1=Hughes |first1=Aaron W. |title=Muslim Identities: An Introduction to Islam |date=2013 |publisher=Columbia University Press |isbn=978-0-231-53192-4 |page=108 |url={{GBurl|95jSBFFaDkUC|p=108}} |oclc=833763900 }}</ref><ref>{{cite book |last1=Barton |first1=Simon |title=A History of Spain|publication-place=Basingstoke|date=2004 |publisher=Palgrave Macmillan |isbn=978-0-333-63257-4|page=57|edition=1st|lccn=2003066374|oclc=58477225}}</ref><ref>{{cite book |last=Chejne|oclc=433841628 |first=Anwar G. |year=1974 |title=Muslim Spain: Its History and Culture |lccn=73-87254|publication-place=Minneapolis, Minnesota|publisher=University of Minnesota Press |isbn=978-0-8166-0688-7 |pages=43–49}}</ref> with most of present-day Portugal ruled by the [[Taifa of Badajoz]], then in 1022 by the [[Taifa of Seville]]. These were conquered by the [[Almoravids]] in 1086, then by the [[Almohads]] in 1147.<ref>{{cite book|pages=55–58|isbn=978-0-511-65027-7|publication-place=Cambridge|oclc=559058693|publisher=Cambridge University Press|first=Anthony R.|last=Disney|url=https://fenix.iseg.ulisboa.pt/downloadFile/1126033050836544/A.%20R.%20Disney%20-%20A%20History%20of%20Portugal%20and%20the%20Portuguese%20Empire_%20From%20Beginnings%20to%201807%20%28Volume%201%29%20%282009%29.pdf|publication-date=June 2009|access-date=18 April 2026|title=A History of Portugal and the Portuguese Empire|volume=1: Portugal|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20251116214058/https://fenix.iseg.ulisboa.pt/downloadFile/1126033050836544/A.%20R.%20Disney%20-%20A%20History%20of%20Portugal%20and%20the%20Portuguese%20Empire_%20From%20Beginnings%20to%201807%20(Volume%201)%20(2009).pdf|url-status=live|archive-date=16 November 2025|via=[[University of Lisbon]]}}</ref><ref>{{cite book|title=The Almoravid and Almohad Empires|page=2|isbn=9780748646821|series=The Edinburgh History of the Islamic Empires|first=Amira K.|last=Bennison|publication-date=August 2016|publisher=Edinburgh University Press|publication-place=Edinburgh|oclc=10019128555}}</ref> Invasions from the north also occurred in this period, with [[Viking]]s raiding the coast, including Lisbon, between the 9th and 11th centuries.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Scheen |first1=Rolf |title=Vikings raids on the Spanish Peninsula |journal=Militaria. Revista de cultura militar |date=1996 |issue=8 |pages=67–88 |url=https://revistas.ucm.es/index.php/MILT/article/view/MILT9696110067A/3416|publisher=Complutense University of Madrid|publication-place=Madrid|issn=1988-3315|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20250121105534/http://revistas.ucm.es/index.php/MILT/article/download/MILT9696110067A/3416|archive-date=21 January 2025|url-status=live|access-date=18 April 2026}}</ref><ref>{{cite book |last1=Christys |first1=Ann |title=Vikings in the south : voyages to Iberia and the Mediterranean|oclc=906575918 |publication-date=27 August 2015 |doi=10.5040/9781474213790 |isbn=978-1-4742-1379-0 |page=97|publication-place=London|publisher=Bloomsbury Academic|series=Studies in Early Medieval History|edition=1st}}</ref> The Viking raids resulted in the establishment of small [[Norsemen|Norse]] settlements on the coastline between [[Douro]] and [[Minho Province|Minho]].<ref>{{cite book|last2=Barroca |first2=Mário Jorge|title= Mil Anos da Incursão Normanda ao Castelo de Vermoim |trans-chapter=Chapter 8: Viking incursions in northern Portugal|isbn=978-989-8351-97-5 |date=December 2018|publication-place=Porto|doi=10.21747/9789898351975/mil|doi-access=free|hdl-access=free|url=https://ler.letras.up.pt/uploads/ficheiros/16873.pdf|publisher=CITCEM – Transdisciplinary Research Centre for Culture, Space and Memory|chapter=Capítulo 8: As incursões vikings no Norte de Portugal|last1=Marques|first1=André Evangelista|last3=Amaral|first3=Luís Carlos|chapter-url=https://ler.letras.up.pt/uploads/ficheiros/16871.pdf|pages=143–184|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20260212124206/https://ler.letras.up.pt/uploads/ficheiros/16873.pdf|archive-date=12 February 2026|url-status=live|trans-title=Thousand Years of the Norman Raid on Vermoim Castle|hdl=10362/113163 |language=pt-PT|access-date=19 April 2026}}</ref>

=== ''Reconquista'' ===
{{Main|Portugal in the Reconquista}}

[[File:Estatua de Vimara Peres, Oporto, Portugal, 2012-05-09, DD 01.JPG|thumb|left|upright=.60|[[Vímara Peres]]|alt=Low-angle view of a bronze equestrian statue of Vímara Peres holding a tall spear and banner, silhouetted against a wide blue sky with wispy clouds, standing on a stone pedestal above a distant cityscape.]]

The {{lang|pt|[[Reconquista]]}} was a series of military campaigns by northern Iberian Christian polities against Muslim-ruled [[al-Andalus]].<ref>{{Cite book |last=Caraccioli|url=https://ufdc.ufl.edu/AA00076696/00001/epub|access-date=28 April 2026 |first=Mauro José |chapter=Chapter 1: Narratives of Conquest and the Conquest of Narrative |date=2021 |title=Writing the New World |pages=14–38 |series=The Politics of Natural History in the Early Spanish Empire|publication-place=Gainesville, Florida|lccn=2020023369 |publisher=University Press of Florida|doi=10.5744/9781683401988|doi-access=free |jstor=j.ctv1gt9419.6 |hdl=10919/101936 |oclc=1158505674|hdl-access=free|isbn=978-1-68340-170-4|quote=La Reconquista: a 700-year military and cultural campaign against the Moorish Caliphates of Southern Iberia that culminated in the joint reign of Ferdinand of Aragon and Isabella of Castile as Reyes Católicos.}}</ref> It begun in 718 with the [[Battle of Covadonga]] which led to the establishment of the [[Kingdom of Asturias]] following the defeat of the Umayyad forces by [[Pelagius of Asturias|Pelagius]], a Visigothic nobleman.<ref>{{cite book | last=Williams | first=Mark | year=2004 | title=The story of Spain: the dramatic history of Europe's most fascinating country | location=San Mateo, California | publisher=Golden Era Books |isbn=978-0-97069-692-2|oclc=56478430|page=60}}</ref><ref name="V. Livermore, 1969 pp. 32-33">{{harvp|Livermore|1969|pp=32–33}}</ref>

In 868, [[Porto]] was conquered from the Moors by [[Vímara Peres]] on the orders of King [[Alfonso III of Asturias]]; Peres became the first Count of Portugal.<ref>{{multiref2 | {{Cite book|chapter-url=https://www.academia.edu/23317651|chapter=O Conde Vímara Peres|trans-chapter=The Count Vímara Peres|language=pt-PT|pages=195–219|last=de Almeida Fernandes|first=A. |via=Academia.edu|title=Paróquias suevas e dioceses visigóticas|oclc=68410096|isbn=972-9474-11-7|trans-title=Suebi parishes and Visigothic dioceses|access-date=6 July 2020|archive-date=24 June 2021|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210624200942/https://www.academia.edu/23317651|url-status=live|publisher=ROCHA|year=1997}} | {{Cite book |last=Livermore|doi=10.4324/9781003563938-19 |first=Harold |chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=6oaNEQAAQBAJ&dq=vimara%20peres%20868&pg=PT292 |title=The Origins of Spain and Portugal |publication-date=6 December 2024|orig-date=First published 1971|oclc=1473687568|page=292|publication-place=London|chapter=Chapter 15: The Fall of the Old Church of Toledo: Santiago and the Reconquest|edition=1st|publisher=Routledge |isbn=978-1-040-26004-3 |access-date=28 April 2026|via=Google Books}} | {{Cite book |last=Livermore |first=Harold V. |chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=7Cd0bKhByz8C&pg=PA127 |title=Portugal: A Traveller's History|chapter=Chapter 13: Oporto|publication-place=Woodbridge|lccn=2004010337 |date=2004 |publisher=Boydell Press|oclc=55098244|access-date=28 April 2026 |isbn=978-1-84383-063-4 |pages=127 |language=en}} }}</ref> Finding many towns in the region deserted, Peres decided to rebuild and repopulate them.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Martínez Lema |first1=Paulo |title='Ad populandum': toponímia e repovoamento no sul da Galiza alto-medieval |trans-title='Ad populandum': Toponymy and repopulation in early medieval southern Galicia|language=pt |journal=Revista de Filología Románica |date=28 September 2018 |volume=35 |pages=141–165 |doi=10.5209/RFRM.61690 |doi-access=free }}</ref>

With the forced abdication of Alfonso III in 910, the Kingdom of Asturias split into three kingdoms, which were reunited in 924 under the crown of [[Kingdom of León|León]].<ref>{{cite book | last = Collins |first=Roger| year = 1983 | title = Early medieval Spain: unity in diversity, 400-1000 | publisher = St. Martin's Press |location = New York|isbn=9780312224646
|oclc=260135536|page = 238|lccn=83015931 }}</ref> The first County lasted until 1071, when the [[Kingdom of Galicia]] took over the region following the [[Battle of Pedroso]].<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Costa |first1=Avelino De Jesus Da |title=A restauração da diocese de Braga em 1070 |trans-title=The restoration of the Diocese of Braga in 1070 |language=pt |journal=Lusitania Sacra |date=1956 |pages=17–28 Páginas |doi=10.34632/lusitaniasacra.1956.8221 |doi-access=free }}</ref> In 1096, [[Alfonso VI of León]] refounded the county and bestowed it on [[Henry, Count of Portugal|Henry of Burgundy]], who married to Alfonso's [[Legitimacy (family law)|illegitimate]] daughter, [[Theresa, Countess of Portugal|Teresa of León]].<ref>{{cite book | last =Carvalho Correia| first = Francisco |trans-title=The Monastery of Santo Tirso from 978 to 1588: the silhouette of an entity cast upon the ground of a millenary history| publisher = Universidade de Santiago de Compostela: Servizo de Publicacións e Intercambio Científico |title = O Mosteiro de Santo Tirso de 978 a 1588 : a silhueta de uma entidade projectada no chao de uma história milenária|volume=1|hdl=10347/2416| url =https://minerva.usc.gal/rest/api/core/bitstreams/f16911d7-a572-4cfb-a0eb-262421d41df6/content | archive-url =https://web.archive.org/web/20260420150531/https://minerva.usc.gal/rest/api/core/bitstreams/f16911d7-a572-4cfb-a0eb-262421d41df6/content| url-status =live| archive-date =20 April 2026 | year= 2008| publication-place= Santiago de Compostela | isbn = 978-84-9887-038-1| language = pt-PT|page=202|access-date=20 April 2026}}</ref>

=== Independence ===
{{Main|Portugal in the Middle Ages}}

[[File:D. Afonso Henriques - Compendio de crónicas de reyes (Biblioteca Nacional de España).png|thumb|upright 0.6|King [[Afonso I of Portugal|Afonso I]]|alt=Medieval-style illuminated illustration of King Afonso I seated on an ornate throne, holding a sword in his left hand and a golden orb in the right hand, with gold leaf details highlighting the crown, robe, and architectural frame around him.]]

After the death of Count Henry, in 1112, a dispute erupted, culminating in the [[Battle of São Mamede]], in 1128, where [[Afonso I of Portugal|Afonso Henriques]] defeated the forces of his mother [[Teresa, Countess of Portugal|Countess Teresa]] and her lover [[Fernão Peres de Trava]], establishing himself as sole leader of the [[County of Portugal|county]] and achieving {{lang|la|[[de facto]]}} independence.<ref>{{cite book|page=116|year=1973|oclc=1508762402|chapter=Chapter Six: The Emergence of Portugal|volume=1|title=A history of Spain and Portugal|first=Stanley G.|last=Payne|publisher=University of Wisconsin Press|publication-place=Madison, Wisconsin|isbn=0-299-06270-8|url=https://archive.org/details/historyofspainpo00payn|via=Wayback Machine|access-date=20 April 2026}}</ref> Afonso's campaigns were successful, and in 1139, he was victorious in the [[Battle of Ourique]], and took the title of [[King of Portugal]].<ref>{{cite book|page=117|year=1973|oclc=1508762402|chapter=Chapter Six: The Emergence of Portugal|volume=1|title=A history of Spain and Portugal|first=Stanley G.|last=Payne|publisher=University of Wisconsin Press|publication-place=Madison, Wisconsin|isbn=0-299-06270-8|url=https://archive.org/details/historyofspainpo00payn|via=Wayback Machine|access-date=20 April 2026}}</ref> In 1143, Afonso was recognised as Afonso I, king of Portugal, by King [[Alfonso VII of León]], and in 1179 by [[Pope Alexander III]], with the [[papal bull]] {{lang|la|[[Manifestis Probatum]]}}.<ref name="Jenkins Sofos 2023 p145">{{cite book|publication-date=11 July 1996|publisher=Routledge|page=145|edition=1st|isbn=9780415123136|publication-place=London|title=Nation and identity in contemporary Europe|chapter=Chapter 7: Multiple national identities, immigration and racism in Spain and Portugal|first1=Brian|first2=Spyros A.|last1=Jenkins|last2=Sofos|editor-first1=Brian |editor-first2=Spyros A. |editor-last1=Jenkins |editor-last2=Sofos |oclc=33335411|doi=10.4324/9780203208915}}</ref>

Afonso Henriques and his successors, aided by military [[Christian monasticism|monastic orders]], continued pushing south until the [[Portuguese conquest of the Algarve|capture of Algarve]] in 1249.<ref>{{cite journal |last1=Wilson |first1=Jonathan |title='Neither age nor sex sparing': the Alvor massacre 1189, an anomaly in the Portuguese ''Reconquista''? |journal=Journal of Medieval Iberian Studies |date=3 May 2020 |volume=12 |issue=2 |pages=199–229 |doi=10.1080/17546559.2019.1704043 }}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url= https://www.ccdr-alg.pt/site/sites/default/files/recortes/20170216_barlavento_oreinoalgplenodireito.pdf|publisher=CCDR-Alg|title=O Reino do Algarve de pleno direito|language=pt-PT|trans-title=The Kingdom of the Algarve in its own right|first=José António|last=Martins|date=February 16, 2017|access-date=April 10, 2026|archive-date=31 January 2026|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20260131163749/https://www.ccdr-alg.pt/site/sites/default/files/recortes/20170216_barlavento_oreinoalgplenodireito.pdf|url-status=live}}</ref> With minor readjustments, Portugal's borders have largely remained the same since the signing of the [[Treaty of Alcañices|Treaty of Alcanizes]] in 1297, making it one of the oldest established international borders in Europe.<ref>{{cite journal|last=García|first=Eusebio Medina|journal=Revista de estudios extremeños|volume=62|issue=2|pages=713–723|title= Orígenes históricos y ambigüedad de la frontera hispano-lusa (La Raya)|issn=0210-2854|language=es|trans-title=Historical origins and ambiguity of the Hispano-Portuguese border (La Raya)|year=2006|publisher=Centro de Estudios Extremeños|publication-place=Badajoz|url=https://www.dip-badajoz.es/cultura/ceex/reex_digital/reex_LXII/2006/T.%20LXII%20n.%202%202006%20mayo-ag/RV000827.pdf|access-date=20 April 2026|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20260206183123/https://www.dip-badajoz.es/cultura/ceex/reex_digital/reex_LXII/2006/T.%20LXII%20n.%202%202006%20mayo-ag/RV000827.pdf|archive-date=6 February 2026}}</ref> In 1348-49 Portugal, as with the rest of Europe, was devastated by the [[Black Death]].<ref>{{cite thesis|title=A Peste Negra em Portugal Os casos do Entre-Douro-e-Minho e do Entre-Tejo-e-Odiana|hdl=10216/137710|page=678|first=André Filipe|last=Oliveira da Silva|access-date=20 April 2026|url=https://repositorio-aberto.up.pt/bitstream/10216/137710/2/514861.pdf|publisher=University of Porto|publication-place=Porto|year=2021|language=pt-PT|trans-title=The Black Death in Portugal: the cases of Entre-Douro-e-Minho and Entre-Tejo-e-Odiana|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230531014600/https://repositorio-aberto.up.pt/bitstream/10216/137710/2/514861.pdf|archive-date=31 May 2023|url-status=live}}</ref> In 1373, Portugal, during the reign of [[Ferdinand I of Portugal|King Ferdinand I]], in the midst of the [[Fernandine Wars]], made an [[Anglo-Portuguese Alliance|alliance with England]], which would be later strengthened by the signing of the [[Treaty of Windsor (1386)|Treaty of Windsor]],<ref>{{cite thesis |degree=Master of arts in history|last=Winslett |first=Matthew |others=Supervising Professor: Douglas Richmond |title=The Nadir of Alliance: The British Ultimatum of 1890 and Its Place in Anglo-Portuguese Relations, 1147–1945|url=https://mavmatrix.uta.edu/history_theses/66/|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20250412011021/https://mavmatrix.uta.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?params=/context/history_theses/article/1065/type/native/&path_info= |archive-date=12 April 2025 |date=August 2008 |publisher=[[The University of Texas at Arlington]]|publication-place=Arlington, Texas |page=14 |quote=This treaty has been the cornerstone of both nations' relations with each other ever since. This is particularly true in regards to the Portuguese. The various treaties that follow it build upon the promises made at Windsor and never abrogate its terms, especially in the case of guarantees of territorial defense and military aid. |postscript=none|access-date=20 April 2026|url-status=live}}</ref>  making it the oldest standing alliance in the world.<ref>{{cite web |last1=Fergusson |first1=George |last2=Trowbridge |first2=Benjamin |title=History's Unparalleled Alliance: the Anglo-Portuguese Treaty of Windsor, 9th May 1386 |website=History of Government |publisher=gov.uk |date=9 May 2016 |url=https://history.blog.gov.uk/2016/05/09/historys-unparalleled-alliance-the-anglo-portuguese-treaty-of-windsor-9th-may-1386/ |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240607175621/https://history.blog.gov.uk/web/20240607175621/https://history.blog.gov.uk/2016/05/09/historys-unparalleled-alliance-the-anglo-portuguese-treaty-of-windsor-9th-may-1386/ |archive-date= Jun 7, 2024|access-date=20 April 2026}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal |last1=Grose |first1=Clyde L. |title=The Anglo-Portuguese Marriage of 1662 |journal=The Hispanic American Historical Review |date=1930 |volume=10 |issue=3 |pages=313–352 |doi=10.2307/2506378 |jstor=2506378 }}</ref>

=== Age of Discovery ===
{{main||Portuguese Empire}}

[[File:Portuguese Carracks off a Rocky Coast.jpg|thumb|alt=Painting showing 10 Portuguese vessels near a fortified walled town with a tower rising above the harbor where several vessels lie at anchor, wooded hills stretch into the background, and a distant island appears offshore, with colors shifting from brown-green in the foreground to blue in the distance.|The ''Portuguese Carracks Off a Rocky Coast'' painting displays Portuguese vessels of the Discovery Age during the 16th century.]]

In 1383, [[John I of Castile]] and [[Beatrice of Portugal]], the only surviving legitimate child of [[Ferdinand I of Portugal]], claimed the throne of Portugal. [[John I of Portugal|John of Aviz]] led a revolt against them and defeated the Castilians in the [[Battle of Aljubarrota]], with the [[House of Aviz]] becoming the ruling house that led Portugal into the limelight of European politics and culture.<ref>{{cite book|lccn=60-14301|publication-place=Lincoln, Nebraska|oclc=396468|chapter=Chapter Eight: An Old Road and a New|first=Bailey Wallys|last=Diffie|title=Prelude to empire: Portugal overseas before Henry the Navigator|chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=IjBfEorbZWAC&pg=PA73|date= December 1960|publisher=University of Nebraska Press|isbn=978-0-8032-5049-9|pages=73–75|access-date=20 April 2026|via=Google Books}}</ref><ref>{{cite book |last1=Forgeng |first1=Jeffrey L. |pages=47–154 |jstor=10.7722/j.ctt18gzf5h.7 |title=The Book of Horsemanship by Duarte I of Portugal |chapter=The Book of Horsemanship |date=2016 |publisher=Boydell & Brewer |isbn=978-1-78327-103-0 }}</ref><ref name="Carvalho 2014">{{cite journal |last1=Santiago de Carvalho |first1=Mário |title=Uma modernidade perdida. Da melancolia à alegria racional na antropologia do homem superior, segundo D. Duarte |trans-title=A lost modernity. From melancholy to rational joy in the anthropology of superior man, according to D. Duarte |url=https://www.uc.pt/fluc/dfci/public_/publicacoes/uma_modernidade_perdida |date=2013 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20241127100417/https://www.uc.pt/fluc/dfci/public_/publicacoes/uma_modernidade_perdida |archive-date=27 November 2024 |url-status=dead |journal=Revista filosófica de Coimbra |volume=22 |issue=43 |pages=187–206 |language=es |access-date=17 April 2026|issn=0872-0851|eissn=2184-7584|publisher=Instituto de Estudos Filosóficos da Faculdade de Letras da Universidade de Coimbra|publication-place=Coimbra}}</ref> Portugal acquired its first colonies by [[Portuguese conquest of Ceuta|conquering Ceuta]] in 1415.<ref>{{cite book |first=Malyn |last=Newitt |title=A history of Portuguese overseas expansion, 1400-1668|lccn=2004005783 |publication-date=11 November 2004 |publisher=Routledge|oclc=54694601|publication-place=London|isbn=978-1134553044|page=20}}</ref> The country spearheaded the [[Age of Discovery|European exploration of the world]] under the sponsorship of [[Prince Henry the Navigator]],<ref name="portugueseEmpireBook">{{Cite book |last=Russell-Wood |first=A. J. R.  |title=The Portuguese Empire, 1415-1808: A World on the Move|chapter=Chapter 1: Portugal and the 'Age of Discoveries'|date=1998 |publisher=Johns Hopkins University Press|publication-place=Baltimore, Maryland |doi=10.1353/book.77743 |isbn=978-1-4214-4120-7|pages=8–11|oclc=1193105910}}</ref><ref name="Casimiro2020">{{cite journal|last=Casimiro|first=Tânia Manuel|date=2020|title=Globalization, trade, and material culture: Portugal's role in the making of a multicultural Europe (1415–1806)|journal=Post-Medieval Archaeology|issn=0079-4236|eissn=1745-8137|volume=54|issue=1|pages=1–17|doi=10.1080/00794236.2020.1750239 |hdl=10362/98047 |quote=In the Early Modern age, Portugal was among the first European countries to engage in overseas trade and colonial ventures.|publisher=Routledge|publication-place=London}}</ref> and made several seminal advancements in [[Portuguese nautical science|nautical science]].{{efn|The Portuguese devised the {{lang|pt-PT|[[volta do mar]]}} navigational technique, which enabled safe roundtrip voyages in open sea; this was a major turning point in world history, as it meant that voyagers could now return from long distant places, marking a leap from coastal hugging to deep-sea, long-range navigation, which consequently contributed to further advancements in nautical science and cartography, without that discovery European colonial empires could not have been established.<ref>{{Cite book|last=Dartnell|first=Lewis|author-link=Lewis Dartnell|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=5Z9xDwAAQBAJ|access-date=28 April 2026|title=Origins: how Earth's history shaped human history|date=14 May 2019|publisher=Basic Books|publication-place=New York|isbn=978-1-5416-1789-6|pages=218–222|oclc=1101101039|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220303144137/https://books.google.com/books?id=5Z9xDwAAQBAJ|archive-date=3 March 2022|url-status=live}}</ref>}} 

Throughout the 15th century, [[European exploration of Africa#Portuguese|Portuguese explorers]] sailed the coast of Africa, establishing trading posts [[Economic history of Portugal#Expansion of the Portuguese empire (15th and 16th centuries)|for commodities]] that ranged from [[gold]] to [[slavery|slaves]].<ref>{{Cite journal |last1=Axelson |first1=Eric |date=June 1970 |title=Review of The Portuguese Seaborne Empire 1415-1825, by C. R. Boxer|journal=The Geographical Journal |volume=136|publication-place=London |issue=2 |pages=296–298|bibcode=1970GeogJ.136..296A |doi=10.2307/1796339 |jstor=1796339|publisher=The Royal Geographical Society|issn=0016-7398|eissn=1475-4959}}</ref> The [[Treaty of Tordesillas]], signed in 1494, divided the newly located lands outside Europe between Portugal and Spain along a line west of [[Geography of Cape Verde|Cape Verde]].<ref>{{cite book|first=Henry |last=Harrisse |author-link=Henry Harrisse |url=https://archive.org/details/diplomatichisto01harrgoog |title=The Diplomatic History of America: Its First Chapter 1452–1493–1494 |publication-place=London |publisher=B. F. Stevens|publication-date=1897|pages=91–97|access-date=29 April 2026|via=Wayback Machine}}</ref> In 1500, [[Gaspar Corte-Real]] sighted [[Greenland]], chartered by [[João Fernandes Lavrador]] two years prior.<ref>{{cite DCB |first=L.-A. |last=Vigneras |title=Corte-Real, Gaspar |volume=1 |url=http://www.biographi.ca/en/bio/corte_real_gaspar_1E.html|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20260215022400/http://www.biographi.ca/en/bio/corte_real_gaspar_1E.html|archive-date=15 February 2026|url-status=live|access-date=20 April 2026|publication-place=Toronto, Ontario}}</ref>{{efn|It has been conjectured that the Canadian town of [[Portugal Cove-St. Philip's]] was founded by the Portuguese however there little evidence for the claim.<ref>{{cite book|title=
Encyclopedia of Newfoundland and Labrador, volume 4 [Extract: letter P]|volume=4|pages=408–409|url=http://collections.mun.ca/cdm/ref/collection/cns_enl/id/2306|access-date=20 April 2026|year=1993|publisher=Harry Cuff Publications Ltd.|editor-first1=Cyrill F.|editor-first2=Robert|editor-last1=Poole|editor-last2=Cuff|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20251026055616/http://collections.mun.ca/digital/collection/cns_enl/id/2306|archive-date=26 October 2025|url-status=live|publication-place=St. John's, Newfoundland and Labrador}}</ref>}} That same year, [[Discovery of Brazil|Pedro Álvares Cabral landed in Brazil]] and claimed it for Portugal.<ref>{{cite book| last = Vianna| first = Hélio| year = 1994| language = pt-BR| title = História do Brasil: período colonial, monarquia e república| edition = 15th| publisher = Melhoramentos| location = São Paulo|page=44|url=https://archive.org/details/historiadobrasil0000vian|access-date=21 April 2026|via=Wayback Machine|isbn=85-06-01999-0|trans-title=History of Brazil: the colonial period, monarchy, and republic|oclc=1200478084}}</ref><ref>{{cite book| last = Bueno| first = Eduardo| language = pt-BR| title = A viagem do descobrimento: a verdadeira história da expedição de Cabral|trans-title=The Voyage of Discovery: the true history of Cabral's expedition| publisher = Objetiva| location = Rio de Janeiro |oclc=43474618| year = 1998 | isbn = 978-85-7302-202-5|page=45}}</ref><ref>{{cite book| last = Bueno| first = Eduardo| language = pt-BR|trans-title=The Voyage of Discovery: the true history of Cabral's expedition| title = A viagem do descobrimento: a verdadeira história da expedição de Cabral| publisher = Objetiva| location = Rio de Janeiro | year = 1998 |oclc=43474618| isbn = 978-85-7302-202-5|pages=106–108}}</ref> The Portuguese explored the [[Indian Ocean]], established trade routes in most of southern Asia, and sent the first direct European maritime trade and diplomatic missions [[Jorge Álvares|to China]] and [[Nanban trade|Japan]].<ref name="portugueseEmpireBook" /><ref>{{cite book|last=Edmonds|first=Richard Louis|publisher=Cambridge University Press|publication-date=29 August 2002|series=The China Quarterly Special Issues|isbn=9780521524032|oclc=1120603872|title=China and Europe Since 1978: A European Perspective|page=1|chapter=China and Europe since 1978: An Introduction|publication-place=New York}}</ref> Portuguese sailors set out to reach eastern Asia, landing in [[Taiwan]], Japan, [[Timor]], [[Flores]], and the [[Moluccas]].<ref name="portugueseEmpireBook" />{{efn|Although some scholars claim that [[Theory of the Portuguese discovery of Australia|Portugal may have discovered Australia]] in 1521,<ref>{{cite news |last=Perry |location=Sydney|first=Michael |url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-australia-map-idUSSYD3449720070321 |title=Map proves Portuguese discovered Australia: new book |publisher=Reuters |date=21 March 2007 |access-date=31 January 2014 |archive-date=2 January 2014 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140102003059/http://www.reuters.com/article/2007/03/21/us-australia-map-idUSSYD3449720070321 |url-status=live }}</ref> it lacks generally accepted evidence.<ref>{{cite journal|title=Review of The Secret Discovery of Australia: Portuguese Ventures 200 Years before Cook, by Kenneth Gordon McIntyre|pages=186–188|journal=The Journal of Pacific History|volume=14|issue=3|jstor=25168383|first=Robert|last=Langdon|publication-date=1979|issn=0022-3344|eissn=1469-9605|publisher= Taylor & Francis|publication-place=London}}</ref>}} Between 1519 and 1522, [[Ferdinand Magellan]] organised a Spanish [[Magellan expedition|expedition]] to the [[East Indies]] that resulted in the first [[circumnavigation]] of the globe.<ref>{{cite book|first=Gordon|last=Miller|title=Voyages: To the New World and Beyond|page=30|publisher=Douglas & McIntyre; University of Washington Press|publication-place=Vancouver, British Columbia; Seattle, Washington|edition=1st|publication-date=3 August 2011|isbn=978-0-295-99115-3|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=k7H7tgAACAAJ|access-date=28 April 2026|oclc=710044991}}</ref> The [[Treaty of Zaragoza]], signed in 1529, divided the Pacific Ocean between Spain and Portugal.<ref>{{cite book|title=The New Cambridge Modern History|volume=2|page=632|publisher=Cambridge University Press|isbn=9780521345361|editor-first=Geoffrey Rudolph|editor-last=Elton|year=1990|lccn=89009938|edition=2nd|oclc=19722459|publication-place=Cambridge}}</ref>

=== Iberian Union and Restoration ===
{{Main|Iberian Union}}

[[File:Joao IV proclaimed king.jpg|thumb|The coronation of King [[John IV of Portugal|John IV]], in Lisbon]]

The 1578 death of [[Sebastian, King of Portugal|King Sebastian]] in [[Battle of Alcácer Quibir|battle]] and the subsequent 1580 death of his great-uncle, [[Henry, King of Portugal|King Henry]], left the Portuguese throne without a direct heir, precipitating a [[Portuguese succession crisis of 1580|dynastic crisis]].<ref>{{cite book|page=158|publication-place=Cambridge|publisher=Cambridge University Press|publication-date=1969|title=A History Of Portugal|url=https://archive.org/details/in.ernet.dli.2015.152869|access-date=20 April 2026|last=Livermore|first=H. V.|via=Wayback Machine|isbn=9780521095716|oclc=1200570453}}</ref><ref>{{cite book |last1=McMurdo|pages=216–224 |first1=Edward |title=The history of Portugal, from the Commencement of the Monarchy to the Reign of Alfonso III |date=1889 |publisher=Sampson Low, Marston, Searle, & Rivington|location=London |url=https://archive.org/details/historyportugal05mcmugoog/page/n10/mode/2up? |access-date=20 April 2026|via=Wayback Machine}}</ref> [[Philip II of Spain]] claimed the throne on the grounds that his mother, [[Isabella of Portugal]], was a Portuguese princess, and in 1580 he was crowned king of Portugal,<ref>{{cite book|first=António Henrique R. de|last= Oliveira Marques|title=History of Portugal|date=July 1972|page=322|publication-place=New York|publisher=Columbia University Press|isbn=978-0-231-03159-2|volume=1: From Lusitania to Empire|oclc=263625054}}</ref><ref>{{cite book|first=Boris|last=Fausto|title=A Concise History of Brazil|page=40|publication-place=Cambridge|oclc=871200248|isbn=978-1-107-63524-1|publisher=Cambridge University Press|series=Cambridge Concise Histories|year=2014}}</ref> forming a [[Iberian Union|personal union]] of the two kingdoms,{{efn|Portugal continued to be independent during the [[Iberian Union]].<ref>{{cite book |last1=Oliveira Marques |first1=Antonio Henrique R. de|title=History of Portugal |date=1976 |publisher=Columbia University Press|publication-place=New York|oclc=2810850|isbn=978-0-231-08353-9 |url=https://archive.org/details/historyofportuga1976marq/page/n309/mode/2up?|access-date=20 April 2026|via=Wayback Machine|edition=2nd|pages=315–317}}</ref>}} depriving Portugal of an independent foreign policy and leading to its involvement in the [[Eighty Years' War]].<ref>{{cite book |last1=Oliveira Marques |first1=Antonio Henrique R. de|title=History of Portugal |date=1976 |publisher=Columbia University Press|publication-place=New York|edition=2nd|oclc=2810850 |isbn=978-0-231-08353-9 |url=https://archive.org/details/historyofportuga1976marq/page/n309/mode/2up?|access-date=20 April 2026|via=Wayback Machine|page=315}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal |last=Aleixandre Tena |first=Francisca |date=1967 |title=La revolución portuguesa de 1640 |url=https://dialnet.unirioja.es/servlet/articulo?codigo=2529413 |language=es |location=Valencia|journal=Saitabi: Revista de la Facultat de Geografia i Història |issue=17 |pages=95–96 |access-date=20 April 2026 |archive-date=17 January 2024 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240117164113/https://ojs.uv.es/index.php/saitabi/article/download/5572/5331|url-status=dead|issn=0210-9980|eissn=2444-7862|trans-title=The Portuguese revolution of 1640}}</ref> The [[Dutch-Portuguese War]] resulted in the loss of Portugal's Indian Ocean trade monopoly between 1595 and 1663.<ref>{{Cite book |last=Newitt|publication-place=London|edition=1st|publisher=Routledge |first=Malyn |date=5 November 2004 |title=A History of Portuguese Overseas Expansion 1400-1668 |doi=10.4324/9780203324042 |isbn=978-1-134-55304-4|pages=204–212|lccn=2004005783|oclc=1241880368}}</ref>

On 15 December 1640, [[John IV of Portugal|John, Duke of Braganza]], was proclaimed king following an uprising spearheaded by [[Forty Conspirators|disgruntled nobles]] on {{ill|Restoration of the Independence (Portugal)|pt|Restauração da Independência|lt=1 December}}, ending 60 years of the Iberian Union under the [[House of Habsburg]], and beginning the rule of the [[House of Braganza]].<ref>{{cite book|pages=279–280|publication-place=Cambridge|publisher=Cambridge University Press|publication-date=1947|title=A History Of Portugal|url=https://archive.org/details/in.ernet.dli.2015.152869|access-date=20 April 2026|last=Livermore|first=H. V.|via=Wayback Machine}}</ref> Following its defeat in the [[Portuguese Restoration War]], Spain [[Treaty of Lisbon (1668)|recognised]] [[Afonso VI of Portugal|Afonso VI]] as king of Portugal.<ref>{{cite book|page=322|publication-place=Cambridge|publisher=Cambridge University Press|publication-date=1947|title=A History Of Portugal|url=https://archive.org/details/in.ernet.dli.2015.152869|access-date=20 April 2026|last=Livermore|first=H. V.|via=Wayback Machine}}</ref> The reign of [[John V of Portugal|John V]] was marked by an influx of gold into the royal treasury, supplied largely by the [[royal fifth]] and a [[gold rush]] that was  one of the largest movements of people from Europe to an American colony.{{efn|Most estimates place the number of Portuguese migrants to [[Colonial Brazil]] during the gold rush of the 18th century at 600,000.<ref>{{cite book|chapter=Capítulo 3: Presença portuguesa: de colonizadores a imigrantes|page=66|url=https://celp.fflch.usp.br/sites/celp.fflch.usp.br/files/Brasil%20500%20anos%20de%20povoamento.pdf|publication-place=Rio de Janeiro|year=2007|isbn=978-85-240-3940-9|author=Instituto Brasileiro de Geografia e Estatística, Centro de Documentação e Disseminação de Informações|publisher=self-published|title=Brasil: 500 anos de povoamento|language=pt-BR|access-date=20 April 2026|trans-title=Brazil: 500 years of Settlement|trans-chapter=“Chapter 3: Portuguese presence: from colonizers to immigrants|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240706022251/https://celp.fflch.usp.br/sites/celp.fflch.usp.br/files/Brasil%20500%20anos%20de%20povoamento.pdf|archive-date=6 July 2024|url-status=live}}</ref>}}<ref>{{cite book|editor-first=Leslie|editor-last=Bethell|title=The Cambridge history of Latin America|volume=2: Colonial Latin America|publication-place=Cambridge|publisher=Cambridge University Press|page=47|isbn=9780521245166|url={{Google books |plainurl=yes |id=hhNfVshMw64C |page=47 }}|lccn=83-19036|publication-date=6 December 1984|oclc=1102319335}}</ref> Lisbon was struck by a major [[1755 Lisbon earthquake|earthquake on 1 November 1755]].<ref>{{cite book |last1=Maxwell |first1=Kenneth |chapter=Pombal: The Paradox of Enlightenment and Despotism |date=1990|oclc=1249784989 |pages=75–118 |doi=10.1007/978-1-349-20592-9_4 |isbn=978-0-333-43961-6|title=Enlightened Absolutism: Reform and Reformers in Later Eighteenth-Century Europe|publisher=Bloomsbury Academic|series=Problems in Focus|edition=1st|publication-place=London|editor-first=H. M.|editor-last=Scott}}</ref>{{efn|It had an estimated magnitude between 7.7 and 9.0, with casualties ranging from 12,000 to 50,000.<ref>{{cite journal|title=Historical Depictions of the 1755 Lisbon Earthquake|journal=National Information Service for Earthquake Engineering|publisher=University of California, Berkeley|volume=12|url=http://nisee.berkeley.edu/lisbon/index.html |publication-place=Berkeley, California|date=12 November 1998|access-date=6 May 2012 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110311082423/http://nisee.berkeley.edu/lisbon/index.html |archive-date=11 March 2011|url-status=dead|first1=Jan T.|last1=Kozak|first2=Charles D.|last2=James}}</ref>}} Following the earthquake, [[Joseph I of Portugal|King Joseph I]] gave his prime minister [[Sebastião José de Carvalho e Melo, 1st Marquis of Pombal]], overarching powers, with Pombai becoming an [[Enlightened absolutism|enlightened despot]].<ref>{{cite journal|title=Leading by fear and by love: Niccolò Machiavelli and the enlightened despotism of the Marquis of Pombal in the eighteenth century Portugal|doi=10.1080/17449359.2017.1397027|journal=Management & Organizational History|volume=12|issue=4|publication-date=29 October 2017|last=Lopes|first=Miguel Pereira|publisher=Routledge|issn=1744-9359|eissn=1744-9367|pages=374–390|publication-place=London}}</ref>

=== Constitutional monarchy ===
{{Main|History of Portugal (1777–1834)|History of Portugal (1834–1910)}}

[[File:Sessão das Cortes de Lisboa.jpg|thumb|upright|The [[Constituent Cortes of 1820]] approved the first constitution of Portugal.|alt= A large historical painting shows a crowded parliamentary chamber in the middle of a heated debate during the Constituent Cortes of 1820. Dozens of formally dressed men gesture, argue, and rise from their seats beneath towering crystal chandeliers and a richly decorated ceiling, while officials sit at a central desk below a grand canopy and throne.]]

In 1807, Portugal refused [[Napoleon]]'s demand to join the [[Continental System]] of embargo against the United Kingdom; [[Invasion of Portugal (1807)|an invasion]] led by French [[Jean-Andoche Junot|General Junot]] followed, and Lisbon was captured in 1807.<ref>{{cite book |last=Oman | via=Wayback Machine|first=Charles William Chadwick | year=1902 | title=A History of the Peninsular War | volume=I: 1807-1809: From the Treaty of Fontainebleau to the Battle of Corunna|url=https://archive.org/stream/in.ernet.dli.2015.278497/2015.278497 |publisher=Oxford University Press|publication-place=Oxford| url-access=registration | access-date=21 April 2026 |page=7}}</ref><ref>{{cite book | last=Oman | first=Charles William Chadwick | year=1902 | title=A History of the Peninsular War | volume=I: 1807-1809: From the Treaty of Fontainebleau to the Battle of Corunna|publication-place=Oxford|url=https://archive.org/stream/in.ernet.dli.2015.278497/2015.278497 | url-access=registration | access-date=21 April 2026 | publisher=Oxford University Press|pages=26–31| via=Wayback Machine}}</ref> During the [[French invasions of Portugal|Napoleonic invasions]], the [[Transfer of the Portuguese court to Brazil|Portuguese royal family transferred]] the court to [[Rio de Janeiro]], in Brazil, making it the capital between 1808 and 1821.<ref>{{cite journal|journal=Revista do Instituto Histórico e Geográfico Brasileiro|publisher=Instituto Histórico e Geográfico Brasileiro|title=A reordenação urbanística da nova sede da Corte|trans-title=The urban reorganization of the new seat of the Court|language=pt-BR|issn=0101-4366|eissn=2526-1347|issue=436|pages=149–199|first=Nireu Oliveira|last=Cavalcanti|year=2007|url=https://docs.google.com/file/d/1iwf0ZqxRFrcFmWAVH4hT6fhEqywpJoVMP2EqTWLyFULFAJwuei99R69qgPCz|access-date=21 April 2026|publication-place=Rio de Janeiro|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20251007100731/https://drive.usercontent.google.com/download?id=1iwf0ZqxRFrcFmWAVH4hT6fhEqywpJoVMP2EqTWLyFULFAJwuei99R69qgPCz&export=download|archive-date=7 October 2025|url-status=live}}</ref> British intervention in the [[Peninsular War]] helped support Portuguese independence, and all French troops were expelled by 1812.<ref>{{cite book|last=Ertl|oclc=225852394|first=Alan W.|title=Toward an Understanding of Europe: A Political Economic Precis of Continental Integration|year=2008|publisher=Universal Publishers, Inc.|publication-place=Boca Raton, Florida|isbn=978-1-59942-983-0|page=303}}</ref>

In 1820, a [[Liberal Revolution of 1820|constitutionalist insurrection]] began in [[Porto]], which forced [[John VI of Portugal|King John VI]] and his court to return to mainland Portugal in 1821. The death of John in 1826 led to a succession crisis. His eldest son, [[Pedro I of Brazil]], briefly succeeded as [[Pedro IV of Portugal]], but neither the Portuguese nor the Brazilians wanted a reunified monarchy. Consequently, Pedro abdicated the Portuguese crown in favour of his 7-year-old daughter, [[Maria II of Portugal|Maria da Glória]].<ref>{{Cite book|pages=65–68|title=Brazil: the forging of a nation, 1798-1852|last=Barman|url-access=registration|first=Roderick J.|chapter=Chapter 3: Stumbling into Independence, 1820-1822|url=https://archive.org/details/brazilforgingofn0000barm|via=Wayback Machine|oclc=612533527|year=1988|publisher=Stanford University Press|publication-place=Stanford, California|isbn=0-8047-1437-1|access-date=21 April 2026}}</ref><ref>{{cite book|title=Portugal: a country study|url=https://tile.loc.gov/storage-services/master/frd/frdcstdy/po/portugalcountrys00sols_0/portugalcountrys00sols_0.pdf|author= Federal Research Division, Library of Congress|publication-place= Washington, D.C.|editor-last=Solsten|editor-first=Eric|series=Area handbook series|publication-date=January 1993|edition=2d|isbn=0-8444-0776-3|issn=1057-5294|access-date=7 December 2025 |chapter-url=https://countrystudies.us/portugal/36.htm|publisher=United States Government Printing Office|chapter=War of the Two Brothers|department=Constitutionalism|oclc=44354987}}</ref>

Dissatisfaction at Pedro's constitutional reforms led the [[Miguelist|"absolutist" faction of landowners and the church]] to proclaim his brother [[Miguel I of Portugal|Miguel]] king in February 1828. This led to the [[Liberal Wars]], in which Pedro forced Miguel to abdicate in favour of Maria and to go into exile in 1834.<ref>{{Cite book |last=Birmingham|publication-place=Cambridge |first=David |author-link=David Birmingham |doi=10.1017/9781108539951|oclc=1029790624 |title=A Concise History of Portugal |date=2018 |pages=116–119|edition=3rd |publisher=Cambridge University Press |isbn=978-1-108-42419-6}}</ref> Under the constitutional monarchy, the country faced economic crises, political instability, and several coups d'état.<ref>{{Cite book |last=Birmingham |first=David |author-link=David Birmingham |doi=10.1017/9781108539951|oclc=1029790624|publication-place=Cambridge |title=A Concise History of Portugal |date=2018 |pages=117–145|edition=3rd |publisher=Cambridge University Press |isbn=978-1-108-42419-6}}</ref> At the same time it expanded its colonies in Africa, but this culminated in the [[1890 British Ultimatum]], which thwarted Portugal's imperialist ambitions and represented a devastating blow to the monarchy.<ref>{{cite journal|journal=The Journal of Modern History|volume=19|issue=1|pages=1–17|title=Portugal and the Partition of Africa|publication-date=March 1947|first=Charles E.|last=Nowell|jstor=1875649|publisher=University of Chicago Press|publication-place=Chicago, Illinois|doi=10.1086/237079 |issn=0022-2801|eissn=1537-5358}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal |last1=Livermore |first1=H.V. |url=https://www.bhsportugal.org/uploads/fotos_artigos/files/LordSalisburysUltimatum.pdf|title=Lord Salisbury's Ultimatum |journal=British Historical Society of Portugal Annual Report|publisher=British Historical Society of Portugal|date=1997 |volume=24 |pages=147–171|access-date=21 April 2026|publication-place=Lisbon|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20250920031545/https://www.bhsportugal.org/uploads/fotos_artigos/files/LordSalisburysUltimatum.pdf|archive-date=20 September 2025|url-status=live}}</ref>

=== First Republic and {{lang|pt-PT|Estado Novo}} ===
{{Main|First Portuguese Republic|Estado Novo (Portugal)}}

[[File:Proclamação República Portuguesa.jpg|thumb|left|[[José Relvas]] proclaiming the republic on [[5 October 1910 revolution|5 October 1910]], at [[Lisbon City Hall]]|alt=Black-and-white photograph of José Relvas proclaiming the Republic together with several formally dressed men leaning over the balcony railing of Lisbon City Hall and looking down onto a square surrounded with buildings.]]

In 1908, [[Carlos I of Portugal|King Carlos I]] and his son and heir, [[Luís Filipe, Prince Royal of Portugal|Luís Filipe, Prince Royal]], [[Lisbon Regicide|were assassinated]] by [[Portuguese Republican Party|republican]] sympathizers.<ref>{{cite book|first=Douglas Lamphier|last=Wheeler|title=Republican Portugal: A Political History, 1910–1926|publisher= University of Wisconsin Press|oclc=462170679|publication-place=Madison, Wisconsin|year=1978|pages=43–45|url=https://archive.org/details/republicanportug0000whee|via=Wayback Machine|access-date=21 April 2026|isbn=0299074501}}</ref> In 1910, the monarchy was [[5 October 1910 revolution|replaced]] with a republic.<ref>{{cite book|oclc=462170679|first=Douglas Lamphier|last=Wheeler|title=Republican Portugal: A Political History, 1910–1926|publisher= University of Wisconsin Press|publication-place=Madison, Wisconsin|year=1978|page=48|url=https://archive.org/details/republicanportug0000whee|via=Wayback Machine|access-date=21 April 2026|isbn=0299074501}}</ref> During [[World War I]], Portugal [[Portugal during World War I|fought for the Allies]]; however, the war hurt its weak economy.<ref>{{cite act |author=Presidency of the Republic |trans-title=Law No. 491 |at=No. 47/1916, Series I|location={{lang|pt-PT|[[Diário da República|Diário do Governo]]}} |url=https://files.diariodarepublica.pt/1s/1916/03/04700/02370237.pdf|access-date=21 April 2026|date=12 March 1916|language=pt-PT|title=Lei n.º 491}}</ref><ref>{{cite report|publisher=Fundação Francisco Manuel dos Santos|author= Comité de Datação dos Ciclos Económicos Portugueses|language=pt-PT|url=https://ffms.pt/sites/default/files/2023-11/1.%201910-1919_vf_0.pdf|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20251013192611/https://ffms.pt/sites/default/files/2023-11/1.%201910-1919_vf_0.pdf|archive-date=13 October 2025|url-status=live|title=Recessões do período 1910-1919: a recessão externa da I Guerra Mundial|trans-title=“Recessions in the period 1910–1919: the external recession of the First World War|access-date=21 April 2026|publication-date=30 May 2023|publication-place=Lisbon}}</ref> Political instability and economic weaknesses created chaos and unrest during the [[First Portuguese Republic|First Republic]].<ref>{{cite journal|journal= e-Journal of Portuguese History|type=Article|url=https://www.brown.edu/Departments/Portuguese_Brazilian_Studies/ejph/html/issue17|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130527151917/http://www.brown.edu/Departments/Portuguese_Brazilian_Studies/ejph/html/issue17/pdf/v9n1a04.pdf|archive-date=27 May 2013|access-date=21 April 2026|hdl=10400.14/18175|issn=1645-6432|publisher=University of Porto & Brown University|last=Sardica|first=José Miguel|year=2011|pages=63–89|volume=9|issue=1|title=The memory of the portuguese First Republic throughout the twentieth century}}</ref> These conditions led to its [[28 May 1926 coup d'état|overthrow]] in 1926 and the establishment of the {{lang|pt-PT|[[Ditadura Nacional]]}}, which [[1933 Portuguese constitutional referendum|evolved]] into the right-wing dictatorship of the {{lang|pt-PT|[[Estado Novo (Portugal)|Estado Novo]]}}, under [[António de Oliveira Salazar]], in 1933.<ref>{{Cite book |chapter=Portugal |year=1998 |title=Longman Companion to European Decolonisation in the Twentieth Century |edition=1st |pages=45–46 |publisher=Routledge |doi=10.4324/9781315845296-16 |isbn=978-1-315-84529-6|publication-place=London|first=Muriel E.|oclc=1307516369|last=Chamberlain}}</ref>

During the {{lang|pt-PT|Estado Novo}} Portugal [[Portugal during World War II|remained neutral]] in [[World War II]].<ref>{{Cite journal|last=Leite|first=Joaquim da Costa|year= 1998|title=Neutrality by Agreement: Portugal and the British Alliance in World War II|url= http://digitalcommons.wcl.american.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1305&context=auilr |journal=[[American University International Law Review]]|volume=14|issue=1|pages= 185–199|access-date=21 April 2026}}</ref> New economic development projects and relocation of mainland citizens into the overseas provinces in Africa were initiated.<ref>{{cite book|pages=161–165|title=Portugal's African wars: Angola, Guinea Bissao, Mozambique|publisher=Third Press|publication-place=New York|isbn=9780893880729|year=1974|last1=Humbaraci|oclc=1253803|last2=Muchnik|first1=Arslan|first2=Nicole|url=https://archive.org/details/PortugalsAfricanWars|via=Wayback Machine|access-date=21 April 2026|lccn=72-93676|url-access=registration}}</ref> However, [[indigenous peoples]] already [[second-class citizen]]s, were sidelined by those development projects, which led to the emergence of independence movements, in the 1960s, culminating in 1961 in the [[Portuguese Colonial War]], which lasted until [[Carnation Revolution|1974]].<ref>{{cite book|title=O «Indígena» no Pensamento Colonial Português – 1895-1961|trans-title=The indigenous peoples in the Portuguese colonial thought – 1895-1961|language=pt-PT|url=https://www.mariomoutinho.pt/images/PDFs/Livros_Cap/2001indigenacolonial.pdf|publisher=Edições Universitárias Lusófonas|publication-date=April 2000|publication-place=Lisbon|isbn=978-972-8296-58-2|lccn=2001354975|first=Mário C.|last=Moutinho|oclc=46959389|edition=1st|access-date=2 February 2026|url-status= live|archive-url= https://web.archive.org/web/20250808102837/https://www.mariomoutinho.pt/images/PDFs/Livros_Cap/2001indigenacolonial.pdf#page=1.00&gsr=0|archive-date=8 August 2025}}</ref><ref>{{cite book|title=Angola 1961 – Da Baixa do Cassange a Nambuangongo|pages=59–64|isbn=9789728816360|publication-place=Lisbon|publication-date=December 2005|publisher=Prefácio|oclc=144216295|first=António Lopes Pires|last=Nunes|language=pt-PT|trans-title=Angola 1961 – From Baixa do Cassange to Nambuangongo|volume=3}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal|journal=CPHRC Working Papers|series=2|article-number=1|first=Stewart|last=Lloyd-Jones|publisher=ISCTE – University Institute of Lisbon|publication-place=Lisbon|publication-date=November 2001|url=https://cd25a.uc.pt/media/pdf/portugal-since-1974.pdf|access-date=21 April 2026|title=Portugal's history since 1974|pages=1–16|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141017041856/http://www.cd25a.uc.pt/media/pdf/portugal-since-1974.pdf|archive-date=17 October 2014|url-status=live}}</ref>

=== Return to democracy ===
{{Main|Carnation Revolution|Third Portuguese Republic}}

[[File:Ondanks een verbod wilden leden van de Portugese Maoïstische beweging MRPP op 31, Bestanddeelnr 927-7289.jpg|thumb|left|Armoured vehicles in Lisbon's [[Rossio Square]], in 1975|alt=Black-and-white nighttime photograph of several armoured military vehicles lined up in Rossio Square, with soldiers standing beside them near the Column of Pedro IV and bright neon signs glowing in the background.]]

On 25 April 1974, the Carnation Revolution overthrew the {{lang|pt-PT|Estado Novo}} beginning the [[Portuguese transition to democracy|transition to democracy]], and the beginning of the dissolution of its colonial empire [[Handover of Macau|until 1999]].<ref>{{multiref2
|1={{cite act |author=National Salvation Junta |trans-title=Suplement|at=No. 97/1974, Series I |location={{lang|pt-PT|[[Diário da República|Diário do Governo]]}} |url=https://files.diariodarepublica.pt/gratuitos/1s/1974/04/09701.pdf|access-date=21 April 2026|date=25 April 1974|language=pt-PT|title=Suplemento}}
|2={{Cite journal |last1=Minter |first1=William |last2=Cann |first2=John P. |date=September 1998 |title=Counterinsurgency in Africa: The Portuguese Way of War, 1961-1974 |journal=African Studies Review |volume=41 |issue=2 |page=182 |doi=10.2307/524850 |jstor=524850|eissn=1555-2462|issn=0002-0206|publisher=Cambridge University Press|publication-place=Cambridge }}
|3={{cite journal|publisher=GIGA German Institute of Global and Area Studies, Institute of Asian Studies in cooperation with the National Institute of Chinese Studies, White Rose East Asia Centre at the Universities of Leeds and Sheffield and Hamburg University Press|journal=Journal of Current Chinese Affair|last1=Chung|first1=Thomas|last2=Tieben|first2= Hendrik|eissn=1868-4874|issn=1868-1026|title=Macau: Ten Years after the Handover|pages=7–17|publication-date=1 March 2009|type=Research article|doi=10.1177/186810260903800102|volume=38|issue=1|doi-access=free}}}}</ref> Portugal was governed by a succession of provisional governments, with a lot of social and political tension, during the [[Ongoing Revolutionary Process]] (PREC), until the [[1976 Portuguese legislative election|legislative election of 1976]].<ref>{{cite book|pages=147–160|title=The making of Portuguese democracy|first=Kenneth|last=Maxwell|publisher=Cambridge University Press|oclc=1531761051|publication-place=Cambridge|year=1995|isbn=9780521460774}}</ref> 

After the transition to democracy, Portugal fluctuated between [[socialism]] and [[neoliberalism]].<ref>{{cite book|pages=160–167|title=The making of Portuguese democracy|oclc=1531761051|publication-place=Cambridge|first=Kenneth|last=Maxwell|publisher=Cambridge University Press|year=1995|isbn=9780521460774}}</ref> In 1986, Portugal joined the [[European Economic Community]] (EEC), which led to a considerable growth of its economy.<ref>{{cite book|pages=177–179|title=The making of Portuguese democracy|first=Kenneth|oclc=1531761051|publication-place=Cambridge|last=Maxwell|publisher=Cambridge University Press|year=1995|isbn=9780521460774}}</ref> The [[2010–2014 Portuguese financial crisis]] led to an international bailout and intense [[austerity]] policies, resulting in lasting social impacts.<ref>{{cite book|url=http://library.fes.de/pdf-files/id/10722.pdf|last=Pedroso|first=Paulo|type=Study|publication-date=April 2014|isbn=978-3-86498-840-0|publication-place=Berlin|publisher=Friedrich-Ebert-Stiftung|oclc=915483230|title=Portugal and the global crisis: the impact of austerity on the economy, the social model and the performance of the state|access-date=21 April 2026}}</ref><ref>{{cite journal|doi=10.1007/s10826-023-02650-9|doi-access=free|title=Families Navigating Macroeconomic Hard Times: The Experiences of Portuguese Emerging Adults and Their Parents in the Aftermath of the Great Recession|journal=Journal of Child and Family Studies|volume=33|issue=1|pages=314–326|publication-date=31 August 2023|issn=1573-2843|first1=Gabriela|last1=Fonseca|first2=Daniela|last2=Lourenço|first3=Rita|last3=Francisco|first4=Carla|last4=Crespo|first5=Ana Paula|last5=Relvas|url=https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/s10826-023-02650-9.pdf|access-date=21 April 2026|publisher=Springer US|type=Original Paper|publication-place=New York}}</ref>

== Geography ==
<!-- If you want to expand this section, please add new info into the main articles: "Geography of Portugal" and "Protected areas of Portugal", your work there will be very appreciated. -->
{{Main|Geography of Portugal}}

[[File:Portugal topographic map-en.svg|thumb|upright|A [[topographic map]] of Portugal]]

Portugal comprises a mainland territory located on the [[Iberian Peninsula]] in [[Southwestern Europe]], commonly referred to as [[Continental Portugal]], as well as the two archipelagos of [[Madeira]] and the [[Azores]] on the [[Atlantic Ocean]]. The country is situated between latitudes 30° and 42° N, and longitudes 32° and 6° W.<ref>{{multiref2 |{{cite web|author=Direção-Geral do Território|publisher=self-published|title=Infraestrutura Geodésica: Portugal Continental|trans-title=Geodetic Infrastructure: Continental Portugal|language=pt-PT|location= Lisbon|date=18 February 2026|access-date=22 February 2026|url=https://www.dgterritorio.gov.pt/sites/default/files/ficheiros-geodesia/RGN_PTTM06_ETRS89.xls|url-status= live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20260219044929/https://www.dgterritorio.gov.pt/sites/default/files/ficheiros-geodesia/RGN_PTTM06_ETRS89.xls|archive-date= 19 February 2026}}|{{cite web|author=Direção-Geral do Território|publisher=self-published|title=Infraestrutura Geodésica: Açores|trans-title=Geodetic Infrastructure: Azores|language=pt-PT|location= Lisbon|date=18 February 2026|access-date=22 February 2026|url=https://www.dgterritorio.gov.pt/sites/default/files/ficheiros-geodesia/RGN_A%C3%A7ores_PTRA08_ITRF93.xlsx|url-status= live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20260219044929/https://www.dgterritorio.gov.pt/sites/default/files/ficheiros-geodesia/RGN_A%C3%A7ores_PTRA08_ITRF93.xlsx|archive-date= 19 February 2026}}|{{cite web|author=Direção-Geral do Território|publisher=self-published|title=Infraestrutura Geodésica: Madeira|trans-title=Geodetic Infrastructure: Madeira|language=pt-PT|location= Lisbon|date=18 February 2026|access-date=22 February 2026|url=https://www.dgterritorio.gov.pt/sites/default/files/ficheiros-geodesia/RGN_Madeira_PTRA08_ITRF93.xlsx|url-status= live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20260219044929/https://www.dgterritorio.gov.pt/sites/default/files/ficheiros-geodesia/RGN_Madeira_PTRA08_ITRF93.xlsx|archive-date= 19 February 2026}}}}</ref> Portugal encompasses a total area of {{convert|156,597|km2|sqmi|0|abbr=on}}, of which {{convert|64,372|km2|sqmi|0|abbr=on}} consists of [[territorial sea]] and [[internal waters]], while the country's land area amounts to {{convert|92,225|km2|sqmi|0|abbr=on}}.<ref name="dgterritorio.gov.pt" /><ref name="dgrm.pt" />{{efn|The territory of Portugal includes its land, [[internal waters]], and [[territorial waters#Territorial sea|territorial sea]].<ref>{{cite web|url=https://diariodarepublica.pt/dr/lexionario/termo/mar-territorial|title=Lexionário - Mar territorial|language=pt-PT|trans-title=Lexicon — Territorial Sea|website=Diário da República|access-date=22 February 2026|author=Diário da República|publisher=Imprensa Nacional – Casa da Moeda, S.
